#### [5.9.2.](#section-5.9.2){.section-number .selfRef} [EST CSR Attributes](#name-est-csr-attributes){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-est-csr-attributes}

Automated bootstrapping occurs without local administrative
configuration of the pledge. In some deployments, it is plausible that
the pledge generates a certificate request containing only identity
information known to the pledge (essentially the X.509 IDevID
information) and ultimately receives a certificate containing
domain-specific identity information. Conceptually, the CA has complete
control over all fields issued in the end-entity certificate.
Realistically, this is operationally difficult with the current status
of PKI CA deployments, where the CSR is submitted to the CA via a number
of non-standard protocols. Even with all standardized protocols used, it
could operationally be problematic to expect that service-specific
certificate fields can be created by a CA that is likely operated by a
group that has no insight into different network services/protocols
used. For example, the CA could even be
outsourced.[¶](#section-5.9.2-1){.pilcrow}

To alleviate these operational difficulties, the pledge [MUST]{.bcp14}
request the EST \"CSR Attributes\" from the EST server, and the EST
server needs to be able to reply with the attributes necessary for use
of the certificate in its intended protocols/services. This approach
allows for minimal CA integrations, and instead, the local
infrastructure (EST server) informs the pledge of the proper fields to
include in the generated CSR (such as rfc822Name). This approach is
beneficial to automated bootstrapping in the widest number of
environments.[¶](#section-5.9.2-2){.pilcrow}

In networks using the BRSKI enrolled certificate to authenticate the
ACP, the EST CSR Attributes [MUST]{.bcp14} include the ACP domain
information fields defined in \[[RFC8994](#RFC8994){.xref}\], [Section
6.2.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8994#section-6.2.2){.relref}.[¶](#section-5.9.2-3){.pilcrow}

The registrar [MUST]{.bcp14} also confirm that the resulting CSR is
formatted as indicated before forwarding the request to a CA. If the
registrar is communicating with the CA using a protocol such as full
Certificate Management over CMS (CMC), which provides mechanisms to
override the CSR Attributes, then these mechanisms [MAY]{.bcp14} be used
even if the client ignores the guidance for the CSR
Attributes.[¶](#section-5.9.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#section-5.9.3 .section}
#### [5.9.3.](#section-5.9.3){.section-number .selfRef} [EST Client Certificate Request](#name-est-client-certificate-requ){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-est-client-certificate-requ}

The pledge [MUST]{.bcp14} request a new Client Certificate; see
\[[RFC7030](#RFC7030){.xref}\], [Section
4.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7030#section-4.2){.relref}.[¶](#section-5.9.3-1){.pilcrow}
:::

::: {#section-5.9.4 .section}
#### [5.9.4.](#section-5.9.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Enrollment Status Telemetry](#name-enrollment-status-telemetry){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-enrollment-status-telemetry}

For automated bootstrapping of devices, the administrative elements that
provide bootstrapping also provide indications to the system
administrators concerning device life-cycle status. This might include
information concerning attempted bootstrapping messages seen by the
client. The MASA provides logs and the status of credential enrollment.
Since an end user is assumed per \[[RFC7030](#RFC7030){.xref}\], a final
success indication back to the server is not included. This is
insufficient for automated use cases.[¶](#section-5.9.4-1){.pilcrow}

The client [MUST]{.bcp14} send an indicator to the registrar about its
enrollment status. It does this by using an HTTP POST of a JSON
dictionary with the attributes described below to the new EST endpoint
at \"/.well-known/brski/enrollstatus\".[¶](#section-5.9.4-2){.pilcrow}

When indicating a successful enrollment, the client [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
first re-establish the EST TLS session using the newly obtained
credentials. TLS 1.3 supports doing this in-band, but TLS 1.2 does not.
The client [SHOULD]{.bcp14} therefore always close the existing TLS
connection and start a new one, using the same Join
Proxy.[¶](#section-5.9.4-3){.pilcrow}

In the case of a failed enrollment, the client [MUST]{.bcp14} send the
telemetry information over the same TLS connection that was used for the
enrollment attempt, with a Reason string indicating why the most recent
enrollment failed. (For failed attempts, the TLS connection is the most
reliable way to correlate server-side information with what the client
provides.)[¶](#section-5.9.4-4){.pilcrow}

The version and status fields [MUST]{.bcp14} be present. The Reason
field [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be present whenever the status field is false. In
the case of a SUCCESS, the Reason string [MAY]{.bcp14} be
omitted.[¶](#section-5.9.4-5){.pilcrow}

The reason-context attribute is an arbitrary JSON object (literal value
or hash of values) that provides additional information specific to the
failure to unroll from this pledge. The contents of this field are not
subject to standardization. This is represented by the group-socket
\"\$\$arbitrary-map\" in the CDDL.[¶](#section-5.9.4-6){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-cddl-for-enrollment-status-}

::: {#cddl-enrollstatus}
::: {#section-5.9.4-7.1}
``` {.sourcecode .lang-CDDL}
<CODE BEGINS> file "enrollstatus.cddl"

enrollstatus-post = {
    "version": uint,
    "status": bool,
    ? "reason": text,
    ? "reason-context" : { $$arbitrary-map }
  }
}

<CODE ENDS>
```
:::

[Figure 19](#figure-19){.selfRef}: [CDDL for Enrollment Status
POST](#name-cddl-for-enrollment-status-){.selfRef}
:::

An example status report can be seen below. It is sent with the media
type: application/json[¶](#section-5.9.4-8){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-example-of-enrollment-statu}

::: {#example-enrollstatus}
::: {#section-5.9.4-9.1}
``` {.sourcecode .lang-json}
{
    "version": 1,
    "status":true,
    "reason":"Informative human readable message",
    "reason-context": { "additional" : "JSON" }
}
```
:::

[Figure 20](#figure-20){.selfRef}: [Example of Enrollment Status
POST](#name-example-of-enrollment-statu){.selfRef}
:::

The server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} respond with an HTTP 200 but [MAY]{.bcp14}
simply fail with an HTTP 404 error.[¶](#section-5.9.4-10){.pilcrow}

Within the server logs, the server [MUST]{.bcp14} capture if this
message was received over a TLS session with a matching Client
Certificate.[¶](#section-5.9.4-11){.pilcrow}
:::

::: {#section-5.9.5 .section}
#### [5.9.5.](#section-5.9.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Multiple Certificates](#name-multiple-certificates){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-multiple-certificates}

Pledges that require multiple certificates could establish direct EST
connections to the registrar.[¶](#section-5.9.5-1){.pilcrow}
:::

::: {#section-5.9.6 .section}
#### [5.9.6.](#section-5.9.6){.section-number .selfRef} [EST over CoAP](#name-est-over-coap){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-est-over-coap}

This document describes extensions to EST for the purpose of
bootstrapping remote key infrastructures. Bootstrapping is relevant for
CoAP enrollment discussions as well. The definition of EST and BRSKI
over CoAP is not discussed within this document beyond ensuring proxy
support for CoAP operations. Instead, it is anticipated that a
definition of CoAP mappings will occur in subsequent documents such as
\[[ACE-COAP-EST](#I-D.ietf-ace-coap-est){.xref}\] and that CoAP mappings
for BRSKI will be discussed either there or in future
work.[¶](#section-5.9.6-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#estbase64}
::: {#section-6 .section}
## [6.](#section-6){.section-number .selfRef} [Clarification of Transfer-Encoding](#name-clarification-of-transfer-e){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-clarification-of-transfer-e}

\[[RFC7030](#RFC7030){.xref}\] defines endpoints to include a
\"Content-Transfer-Encoding\" heading and payloads to be base64-encoded
DER \[[RFC4648](#RFC4648){.xref}\].[¶](#section-6-1){.pilcrow}

When used within BRSKI, the original EST endpoints remain base64 encoded
\[[RFC7030](#RFC7030){.xref}\] (as clarified by
\[[RFC8951](#RFC8951){.xref}\]), but the new BRSKI endpoints that send
and receive binary artifacts (specifically,
\"/.well-known/brski/requestvoucher\") are binary. That is, no encoding
is used.[¶](#section-6-2){.pilcrow}

In the BRSKI context, the EST \"Content-Transfer-Encoding\" header field
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} be ignored if present. This header field does not need
to be included.[¶](#section-6-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#reducedsecuritymodes}
::: {#section-7 .section}
## [7.](#section-7){.section-number .selfRef} [Reduced Security Operational Modes](#name-reduced-security-operationa){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-reduced-security-operationa}

A common requirement of bootstrapping is to support less secure
operational modes for support-specific use cases. This section suggests
a range of mechanisms that would alter the security assurance of BRSKI
to accommodate alternative deployment architectures and mitigate
life-cycle management issues identified in [Section
10](#privacyconsiderations){.xref}. They are presented here as
informative (non-normative) design guidance for future standardization
activities. [Section 9](#acpapplicability){.xref} provides
standardization applicability statements for the ANIMA ACP. Other users
would expect that subsets of these mechanisms could be profiled with
accompanying applicability statements similar to the one described in
[Section 9](#acpapplicability){.xref}.[¶](#section-7-1){.pilcrow}

This section is considered non-normative in the generality of the
protocol. Use of the suggested mechanisms here [MUST]{.bcp14} be
detailed in specific profiles of BRSKI, such as in [Section
9](#acpapplicability){.xref}.[¶](#section-7-2){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.1 .section}
### [7.1.](#section-7.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Trust Model](#name-trust-model){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-trust-model}

This section explains the trust relationships detailed in [Section
2.4](#flow){.xref}:[¶](#section-7.1-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-elements-of-brski-trust-mod}

::: {#section-7.1-2.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    +--------+         +---------+    +------------+     +------------+
    | Pledge |         | Join    |    | Domain     |     |Manufacturer|
    |        |         | Proxy   |    | Registrar  |     | Service    |
    |        |         |         |    |            |     | (Internet) |
    +--------+         +---------+    +------------+     +------------+
:::

[Figure 21](#figure-21){.selfRef}: [Elements of BRSKI Trust
Model](#name-elements-of-brski-trust-mod){.selfRef}

[]{.break}

Pledge:
:   The pledge could be compromised and provide an attack vector for
    malware. The entity is trusted to only imprint using secure methods
    described in this document. Additional endpoint assessment
    techniques are [RECOMMENDED]{.bcp14} but are out of scope of this
    document.[¶](#section-7.1-3.2){.pilcrow}
:   

Join Proxy:
:   Provides proxy functionalities but is not involved in security
    considerations.[¶](#section-7.1-3.4){.pilcrow}
:   

Registrar:
:   When interacting with a MASA, a registrar makes all decisions. For
    Ownership Audit Vouchers (see \[[RFC8366](#RFC8366){.xref}\]), the
    registrar is provided an opportunity to accept MASA
    decisions.[¶](#section-7.1-3.6){.pilcrow}
:   

Vendor Service, MASA:
:   This form of manufacturer service is trusted to accurately log all
    claim attempts and to provide authoritative log information to
    registrars. The MASA does not know which devices are associated with
    which domains. These claims could be strengthened by using
    cryptographic log techniques to provide append only, cryptographic
    assured, publicly auditable logs.[¶](#section-7.1-3.8){.pilcrow}
:   

Vendor Service, Ownership Validation:
:   This form of manufacturer service is trusted to accurately know
    which device is owned by which
    domain.[¶](#section-7.1-3.10){.pilcrow}
:   
:::

::: {#pledgeReductions}
::: {#section-7.2 .section}
### [7.2.](#section-7.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Pledge Security Reductions](#name-pledge-security-reductions){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-pledge-security-reductions}

The following is a list of alternative behaviors that the pledge can be
programmed to implement. These behaviors are not mutually exclusive, nor
are they dependent upon each other. Some of these methods enable offline
and emergency (touch-based) deployment use cases. Normative language is
used as these behaviors are referenced in later sections in a normative
fashion.[¶](#section-7.2-1){.pilcrow}

1.  [The pledge [MUST]{.bcp14} accept nonceless vouchers. This allows
    for a use case where the registrar cannot connect to the MASA at the
    deployment time. Logging and validity periods address the security
    considerations of supporting these use
    cases.[¶](#section-7.2-2.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.2-2.1}
2.  [Many devices already support \"trust on first use\" for physical
    interfaces such as console ports. This document does not change that
    reality. Devices supporting this protocol [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} support
    \"trust on first use\" on network interfaces. This is because
    \"trust on first use\" over network interfaces would undermine the
    logging based security protections provided by this
    specification.[¶](#section-7.2-2.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.2-2.2}
3.  [The pledge [MAY]{.bcp14} have an operational mode where it skips
    voucher validation one time, for example, if a physical button is
    depressed during the bootstrapping operation. This can be useful if
    the manufacturer service is unavailable. This behavior
    [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be available via local configuration or physical
    presence methods (such as use of a serial/craft console) to ensure
    new entities can always be deployed even when autonomic methods
    fail. This allows for unsecured
    imprint.[¶](#section-7.2-2.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.2-2.3}
4.  [A craft/serial console could include a command such as \"est-enroll
    \[2001:db8:0:1\]:443\" that begins the EST process from the point
    after the voucher is validated. This process [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
    include server certificate verification using an on-screen
    fingerprint.[¶](#section-7.2-2.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.2-2.4}

It is [RECOMMENDED]{.bcp14} that \"trust on first use\" or any method of
skipping voucher validation (including use of a craft serial console)
only be available if hardware-assisted Network Endpoint Assessment (NEA)
\[[RFC5209](#RFC5209){.xref}\] is supported. This recommendation ensures
that domain network monitoring can detect inappropriate use of offline
or emergency deployment procedures when voucher-based bootstrapping is
not used.[¶](#section-7.2-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.3 .section}
### [7.3.](#section-7.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Registrar Security Reductions](#name-registrar-security-reductio){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-registrar-security-reductio}

A registrar can choose to accept devices using less secure methods. They
[MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be the default behavior. These methods may be
acceptable in situations where threat models indicate that low security
is adequate. This includes situations where security decisions are being
made by the local administrator:[¶](#section-7.3-1){.pilcrow}

1.  [A registrar [MAY]{.bcp14} choose to accept all devices, or all
    devices of a particular type. The administrator could make this
    choice in cases where it is operationally difficult to configure the
    registrar with the unique identifier of each new device
    expected.[¶](#section-7.3-2.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3-2.1}
2.  [A registrar [MAY]{.bcp14} choose to accept devices that claim a
    unique identity without the benefit of authenticating that claimed
    identity. This could occur when the pledge does not include an X.509
    IDevID factory-installed credential. New entities without an X.509
    IDevID credential [MAY]{.bcp14} form the request per [Section
    5.2](#RequestVoucherFromRegistrar){.xref} using the format per
    [Section 5.5](#RequestVoucherFromMASA){.xref} to ensure the
    pledge\'s serial number information is provided to the registrar
    (this includes the IDevID AuthorityKeyIdentifier value, which would
    be statically configured on the pledge). The pledge [MAY]{.bcp14}
    refuse to provide a TLS Client Certificate (as one is not
    available). The pledge [SHOULD]{.bcp14} support HTTP-based or
    certificate-less TLS authentication as described in EST
    \[[RFC7030](#RFC7030){.xref}\], [Section
    3.3.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7030#section-3.3.2){.relref}.
    A registrar [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} accept unauthenticated new entities
    unless it has been configured to do so by an administrator that has
    verified that only expected new entities can communicate with a
    registrar (presumably via a physically secured
    perimeter.)[¶](#section-7.3-2.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3-2.2}
3.  [A registrar [MAY]{.bcp14} submit a nonceless voucher-request to the
    MASA service (by not including a nonce in the voucher-request). The
    resulting vouchers can then be stored by the registrar until they
    are needed during bootstrapping operations. This is for use cases
    where the target network is protected by an air gap and therefore
    cannot contact the MASA service during pledge
    deployment.[¶](#section-7.3-2.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3-2.3}
4.  [A registrar [MAY]{.bcp14} ignore unrecognized nonceless log
    entries. This could occur when used equipment is purchased with a
    valid history of being deployed in air gap networks that required
    offline vouchers.[¶](#section-7.3-2.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3-2.4}
5.  [A registrar [MAY]{.bcp14} accept voucher formats of future types
    that cannot be parsed by the registrar. This reduces the
    registrar\'s visibility into the exact voucher contents but does not
    change the protocol
    operations.[¶](#section-7.3-2.5){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3-2.5}
:::

::: {#masasecurityreductions}
::: {#section-7.4 .section}
### [7.4.](#section-7.4){.section-number .selfRef} [MASA Security Reductions](#name-masa-security-reductions){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-masa-security-reductions}

Lower security modes chosen by the MASA service affect all device
deployments unless the lower security behavior is tied to specific
device identities. The modes described below can be applied to specific
devices via knowledge of what devices were sold. They can also be bound
to specific customers (independent of the device identity) by
authenticating the customer\'s registrar.[¶](#section-7.4-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#masasecurityreduction_nonce}
::: {#section-7.4.1 .section}
#### [7.4.1.](#section-7.4.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Issuing Nonceless Vouchers](#name-issuing-nonceless-vouchers){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-issuing-nonceless-vouchers}

A MASA has the option of not including a nonce in the voucher and/or not
requiring one to be present in the voucher-request. This results in
distribution of a voucher that may never expire and, in effect, makes
the specified domain an always trusted entity to the pledge during any
subsequent bootstrapping attempts. The log information captures when a
nonceless voucher is issued so that the registrar can make appropriate
security decisions when a pledge joins the domain. Nonceless vouchers
are useful to support use cases where registrars might not be online
during actual device deployment.[¶](#section-7.4.1-1){.pilcrow}

While a nonceless voucher may include an expiry date, a typical use for
a nonceless voucher is for it to be long lived. If the device can be
trusted to have an accurate clock (the MASA will know), then a nonceless
voucher CAN be issued with a limited
lifetime.[¶](#section-7.4.1-2){.pilcrow}

A more typical case for a nonceless voucher is for use with offline
onboarding scenarios where it is not possible to pass a fresh
voucher-request to the MASA. The use of a long-lived voucher also
eliminates concern about the availability of the MASA many years in the
future. Thus, many nonceless vouchers will have no expiry
dates.[¶](#section-7.4.1-3){.pilcrow}

Thus, the long-lived nonceless voucher does not require proof that the
device is online. Issuing such a thing is only accepted when the
registrar is authenticated by the MASA and the MASA is authorized to
provide this functionality to this customer. The MASA is
[RECOMMENDED]{.bcp14} to use this functionality only in concert with an
enhanced level of ownership tracking, the details of which are out of
scope for this document.[¶](#section-7.4.1-4){.pilcrow}

If the pledge device is known to have a real-time clock that is set from
the factory, use of a voucher validity period is
[RECOMMENDED]{.bcp14}.[¶](#section-7.4.1-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#masasecurityreduction_tofu}
::: {#section-7.4.2 .section}
#### [7.4.2.](#section-7.4.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Trusting Owners on First Use](#name-trusting-owners-on-first-us){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-trusting-owners-on-first-us}

A MASA has the option of not verifying ownership before responding with
a voucher. This is expected to be a common operational model because
doing so relieves the manufacturer providing MASA services from having
to track ownership during shipping and throughout the supply chain, and
it allows for a very low overhead MASA service. A registrar uses the
audit-log information as an in-depth defense strategy to ensure that
this does not occur unexpectedly (for example, when purchasing new
equipment, the registrar would throw an error if any audit-log
information is reported). The MASA [SHOULD]{.bcp14} verify the
prior-signed-voucher-request information for pledges that support that
functionality. This provides a proof-of-proximity check that reduces the
need for ownership verification. The proof-of-proximity comes from the
assumption that the pledge and Join Proxy are on the same link-local
connection.[¶](#section-7.4.2-1){.pilcrow}

A MASA that practices TOFU for registrar identity may wish to annotate
the origin of the connection by IP address or netblock and restrict
future use of that identity from other locations. A MASA that does this
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} take care to not create nuisance situations for itself
when a customer has multiple registrars or uses outgoing IPv4-to-IPv4
NAT (NAT44) connections that change
frequently.[¶](#section-7.4.2-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#masasecurityreduction_newanchor}
::: {#section-7.4.3 .section}
#### [7.4.3.](#section-7.4.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Updating or Extending Voucher Trust Anchors](#name-updating-or-extending-vouch){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-updating-or-extending-vouch}

This section deals with two problems: A MASA that is no longer available
due to a failed business and a MASA that is uncooperative to a secondary
sale.[¶](#section-7.4.3-1){.pilcrow}

A manufacturer could offer a management mechanism that allows the list
of voucher verification trust anchors to be extended.
\[[YANG-KEYSTORE](#I-D.ietf-netconf-keystore){.xref}\] describes one
such interface that could be implemented using YANG. Pretty much any
configuration mechanism used today could be extended to provide the
needed additional update. A manufacturer could even decide to install
the domain CA trust anchors received during the EST \"cacerts\" step as
voucher verification anchors. Some additional signals will be needed to
clearly identify which keys have voucher validation authority from among
those signed by the domain CA. This is future
work.[¶](#section-7.4.3-2){.pilcrow}

With the above change to the list of anchors, vouchers can be issued by
an alternate MASA. This could be the previous owner (the seller) or some
other trusted third party who is mediating the sale. If it is a third
party, the seller would need to take steps to introduce the third-party
configuration to the device prior to disconnection. The third party
(e.g., a wholesaler of used equipment) could, however, use a mechanism
described in [Section 7.2](#pledgeReductions){.xref} to take control of
the device after receiving it physically. This would permit the third
party to act as the MASA for future onboarding actions. As the IDevID
certificate probably cannot be replaced, the new owner\'s registrar
would have to support an override of the MASA
URL.[¶](#section-7.4.3-3){.pilcrow}

To be useful for resale or other transfers of ownership, one of two
situations will need to occur. The simplest is that the device is not
put through any kind of factory default/reset before going through
onboarding again. Some other secure, physical signal would be needed to
initiate it. This is most suitable for redeploying a device within the
same enterprise. This would entail having previous configuration in the
system until entirely replaced by the new owner, and it represents some
level of risk.[¶](#section-7.4.3-4){.pilcrow}

For the second scenario, there would need to be two levels of factory
reset. One would take the system back entirely to manufacturer state,
including removing any added trust anchors, and the other (more commonly
used) one would just restore the configuration back to a known default
without erasing trust anchors. This weaker factory reset might leave
valuable credentials on the device, and this may be unacceptable to some
owners.[¶](#section-7.4.3-5){.pilcrow}

As a third option, the manufacturer\'s trust anchors could be entirely
overwritten with local trust anchors. A factory default would never
restore those anchors. This option comes with a lot of power but is also
a lot of responsibility: if access to the private part of the new
anchors are lost, the manufacturer may be unable to
help.[¶](#section-7.4.3-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
